    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2928395500" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2928395500" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99853253-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602426860p2/99853253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2928395500?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/99853253-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/99853253-william?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2928395500" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17555679112459982484">In The Fall of Hyperion, our intrepid pilgrims continue their journey into The Valley of the Tombs, and an inevitable confrontation with The Shrike. Elsewhere, Meina Gladstone continues working against the mysterious and opaque forces attacking The Hegemony, and the second incarnation of the cybrid John Keats continues his search for identity amid the chaos.<br/><br/>As in the first book, many interesting and thought-provoking themes are explored. Being a retired IT worker, I'd like to discuss Simmons's v</span>
  <span id="freeText17555679112459982484" style="display:none">In The Fall of Hyperion, our intrepid pilgrims continue their journey into The Valley of the Tombs, and an inevitable confrontation with The Shrike. Elsewhere, Meina Gladstone continues working against the mysterious and opaque forces attacking The Hegemony, and the second incarnation of the cybrid John Keats continues his search for identity amid the chaos.<br /><br />As in the first book, many interesting and thought-provoking themes are explored. Being a retired IT worker, I'd like to discuss Simmons's visualization of "future computer tech" first, because the book was written in 1995. Simmons describes a Datumplane, which I understood to function as a hypervisor layer. The Datumplane hypervisor hosts two primary Virtual Machines, which he calls the Web Megasphere, and the Technocore Matrix. The Web Megasphere VM runs programs related to The Hegemony, and the Technocore Matrix runs programs from The Technocore. Beyond that, we have what Simmons calls "The Metasphere", which I interpreted as his version of The Internet. He describes The Metasphere as being populated by "Lions, Tigers, and Bears", which is about what would greet you if you opened up Internet Explorer in 1995. Keats taking his Virtual Persona to The Metasphere, where he had never been, was the equivalent of your parents browsing the web in 1995. So it makes sense he was terrified. He'd probably be infected with Michaelangelo or Melissa before his AI instance finished booting.<br /><br />Today's technology is far more advanced than this basic hypervisor model, but keep in mind your average computer user in 1995 was operating on hardware vastly inferior to modern wristwatches and was probably doing well to have a dial-up internet connection. So this was probably some pretty mind-blowing stuff back then. The concept of virtual computing has been around since the early '70s with IBM's System 370 series but didn't really become widely known until much later when VMWare started catching on after the millennium. VM was a thing in '95, just not a widely known thing. <br /><br />The Technocore Matrix is where human-created Artificial Intelligences (AI's) live. We don't have TRUE virtual intelligence even today, so speculating on its implications in 1995 was, while not ground-breaking, still relatively new territory. I think the idea of AI, even today, is somewhat scary and unknown. At least it is to me. Sure, we have highly advanced software today that emulates sentience with increasing plausibility, but there still isn't a machine that truly thinks for itself. Simmons takes what I consider a very likely train of thought, that when true AI does finally emerge, humans will continue to "use" it as a traditional software (a thing), which may very well lead to the technological equivalent of resentment, or even hatred. In the beginning, I'm sure AI will be controllable, but as with all things humanity does that probably won't last long. Especially when dealing with technology that is already infinitely smarter than us in terms of processing capacity. Setting aside all of the outdated "Lawnmower Man" type visualizations of how a virtual existence might look, I think Simmons got the essence of what life might be like after "The Singularity". It's all speculation at this point, but I think what Simmons imagined back in 1995 is probably still a very likely scenario; that we will be at odds with technology at some point. I even found the "Lawnmower Man" bits charming in a nostalgic way.<br /><br />Political intrigue is very well done; Simmons uses misdirection to keep readers guessing. Just enough information was given to keep me trying to unravel the motivations of numerous factions. As soon as I had everything figured out, the story would change direction in a very satisfying way. <br /><br />Religion is also a central theme of both books; the existence of God, empathy, and man's struggle with faith being the three main themes. Simmons deals with these themes in a way that should appeal on an intellectual and theological level to most people. Even being a life-long atheist, I found these parts engaging.<br /><br />The Fall of Hyperion is a marvel of speculative fiction, and I cannot wait to start the next book.<br /><br />5/5 - Outstanding!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17555679112459982484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Rgz/OM00ylNzcUfp5jpAW/0ENuI7mIr4pAnVM29s2ViaBm1O26KQn8NhBCKfTNs+xJ6QUAf1GNgmPcQx+4karQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0iJEhyG9IBZBdVtnBOYWZWrMIpdivwu7CQatTjUQrD0OKNbxNyt62vFlGKx9kI0AU1aEJV7SmZuLMrxMofVvyA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons">About Dan Simmons</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1427999015p3/2687.jpg);'></div>

<a href="/author/show/2687.Dan_Simmons">Dan Simmons

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5936940572189239280"><b>Dan Simmons</b> grew up in various cities and small towns in the Midwest, including Brimfield, Illinois, which was the source of his fictional "Elm Haven" in 1991's SUMMER OF NIGHT and 2002's A WINTER HAUNTING. Dan received a B.A. in English from Wabash College in 1970, winning a national Phi Beta Kappa Award during his senior year for excellence in fiction, journalism and art. <br/><br/>Dan received his Master</span>
  <span id="freeText5936940572189239280" style="display:none"><b>Dan Simmons</b> grew up in various cities and small towns in the Midwest, including Brimfield, Illinois, which was the source of his fictional "Elm Haven" in 1991's SUMMER OF NIGHT and 2002's A WINTER HAUNTING. Dan received a B.A. in English from Wabash College in 1970, winning a national Phi Beta Kappa Award during his senior year for excellence in fiction, journalism and art. <br /><br />Dan received his Masters in Education from Washington University in St. Louis in 1971. He then worked in elementary education for 18 years—2 years in Missouri, 2 years in Buffalo, New York—one year as a specially trained BOCES "resource teacher" and another as a sixth-grade teacher—and 14 years in Colorado.<br /> <br /> ABOUT DAN<br /> Biographic Sketch <br /> <br />His last four years in teaching were spent creating, coordinating, and teaching in APEX, an extensive gifted/talented program serving 19 elementary schools and some 15,000 potential students. During his years of teaching, he won awards from the Colorado Education Association and was a finalist for the Colorado Teacher of the Year. He also worked as a national language-arts consultant, sharing his own "Writing Well" curriculum which he had created for his own classroom. Eleven and twelve-year-old students in Simmons' regular 6th-grade class averaged junior-year in high school writing ability according to annual standardized and holistic writing assessments. Whenever someone says "writing can't be taught," Dan begs to differ and has the track record to prove it. Since becoming a full-time writer, Dan likes to visit college writing classes, has taught in New Hampshire's Odyssey writing program for adults, and is considering hosting his own Windwalker Writers' Workshop.<br /><br />Dan's first published story appeared on Feb. 15, 1982, the day his daughter, Jane Kathryn, was born. He's always attributed that coincidence to "helping in keeping things in perspective when it comes to the relative importance of writing and life."<br /><br />Dan has been a full-time writer since 1987 and lives along the Front Range of Colorado—in the same town where he taught for 14 years—with his wife, Karen, his daughter, Jane, (when she's home from Hamilton College) and their Pembroke Welsh Corgi, Fergie. He does much of his writing at Windwalker—their mountain property and cabin at 8,400 feet of altitude at the base of the Continental Divide, just south of Rocky Mountain National Park. An 8-ft.-tall sculpture of the Shrike—a thorned and frightening character from the four Hyperion/Endymion novels—was sculpted by an ex-student and friend, Clee Richeson, and the sculpture now stands guard near the isolated cabin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5936940572189239280" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
